## Chapter: Chapter 4: Theta Functions and Their Applications

### Introduction

In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of theta functions and their applications in geometric combinatorics. Theta functions, named after the Greek letter 'θ', are mathematical functions that have been studied extensively in number theory, combinatorics, and physics. They are particularly useful in the study of geometric combinatorics due to their ability to encode and decode information about geometric objects.

Theta functions are defined as the ratio of two determinants, and they have a rich history in mathematics. They were first introduced by the German mathematician Carl Friedrich Gauss in the early 19th century, and have since been studied by many other mathematicians, including Peter Gustav Lejeune Dirichlet, Bernhard Riemann, and Hermann Minkowski.

In this chapter, we will explore the properties of theta functions, including their periodicity, symmetry, and transformation properties. We will also discuss how these properties can be used to solve various problems in geometric combinatorics. For example, theta functions can be used to count the number of lattice points in a given region, to determine the number of solutions to a system of linear equations, and to calculate the volume of a polyhedron.

We will also discuss the applications of theta functions in other areas of mathematics, such as elliptic curves, modular forms, and quantum mechanics. These applications demonstrate the wide range of mathematical phenomena that can be understood through the lens of theta functions.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of theta functions and their applications, and you will be equipped with the tools to explore these topics further. So, let's embark on this mathematical journey together, and discover the beauty and power of theta functions.




### Subsection: 3.2c Relevance to Scott’s Problem

The paper "On the Number of Directions Determined by a Three-Dimensional Points Set" by Pach, János, Rom Pinchasi, and Micha Sharir has significant relevance to Scott's Problem. The authors' work has provided a deeper understanding of the structure of directional graphs and trees, which are crucial for solving Scott's Problem.

#### Relevance of Directional Graphs and Trees

The directional graph and tree are fundamental concepts in the study of Scott's Problem. The directional graph, with its vertices representing points in three-dimensional space and edges representing directions between these points, provides a visual representation of the directions determined by a points set. The directional tree, a subset of the directional graph, represents the connected components of the graph and provides a more manageable structure for understanding the number of distinct directions.

The authors' work has shed light on the properties of these structures, which are crucial for solving Scott's Problem. For instance, they have shown that the directional graph is always connected, and that the directional tree is a spanning tree of the directional graph. These properties are key to understanding the structure of the directional graph and tree, and to deriving the number of distinct directions in three-dimensional space.

#### Relevance of the Solution to Scott's Problem

The solution to Scott's Problem, provided by the authors, is based on the properties of the directional graph and tree. The authors have shown that the number of distinct directions in three-dimensional space is equal to the number of vertices in the directional tree. This solution provides a formula for the number of distinct directions, which can be used to solve Scott's Problem.

The authors' work has also provided insights into the complexity of Scott's Problem. They have shown that the problem is NP-hard, meaning that it is computationally difficult to solve for large instances. This has implications for the practical application of Scott's Problem in various fields, such as computer graphics and robotics.

In conclusion, the paper "On the Number of Directions Determined by a Three-Dimensional Points Set" by Pach, János, Rom Pinchasi, and Micha Sharir has made significant contributions to the field of geometric combinatorics, particularly in the study of Scott's Problem. Their work has provided a deeper understanding of the structure of directional graphs and trees, and has led to a solution for Scott's Problem.

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of geometric combinatorics, specifically focusing on Scott's Problem about the number of distinct directions in three-space. We have explored the intricacies of this problem, its implications, and the various approaches that have been used to solve it. The problem, while seemingly simple, has proven to be a challenging one, requiring a deep understanding of geometric principles and combinatorial techniques.

We have seen how the problem can be formulated in terms of directional graphs and trees, and how these structures can be used to enumerate the number of distinct directions. We have also discussed the various algorithms that have been proposed to solve the problem, including the Gauss-Seidel method and the Cameron-Martin theorem. These algorithms, while not providing a definitive solution, have provided valuable insights into the problem and have paved the way for further research.

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of geometric combinatorics, specifically focusing on the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines. This theorem, named after the mathematicians who first proved it, is a fundamental result in the field of combinatorics and has wide-ranging applications in various areas of mathematics.

The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem deals with the problem of determining the number of monochromatic lines in a given coloring of the edges of a complete graph. A monochromatic line is a set of edges that all have the same color. The theorem provides a formula for this number, which is given by the product of the number of vertices and the number of colors minus one, raised to the power of the number of vertices minus one.

This theorem is particularly useful in the study of hypergraphs, which are generalizations of graphs where the edges can have multiple vertices. In fact, the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem can be extended to hypergraphs, providing a powerful tool for understanding the structure of these complex objects.

In this chapter, we will first introduce the necessary background on graphs and hypergraphs, and then proceed to prove the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem. We will also discuss some of its applications and variations, and explore how it relates to other important results in combinatorics.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem and its significance in geometric combinatorics. This knowledge will serve as a foundation for the subsequent chapters, where we will delve deeper into the fascinating world of combinatorics and its applications.




### Subsection: 4.1a Definition and Statement

The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines is a fundamental result in the field of geometric combinatorics. It provides a formula for the number of monochromatic lines in a given coloring of the edges of a complete graph. 

#### 4.1a.1 Definition of Monochromatic Lines

A monochromatic line is a set of edges that all have the same color. In other words, a monochromatic line is a subset of the edges of a graph where all the edges have the same color. This concept is central to the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem, as it is the number of these monochromatic lines that we are trying to determine.

#### 4.1a.2 Statement of the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem

The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem states that the number of monochromatic lines in a given coloring of the edges of a complete graph is given by the product of the number of vertices and the number of colors minus one, raised to the power of the number of vertices minus one. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
\text{Number of monochromatic lines} = (n \cdot (c-1))^{n-1}
$$

where $n$ is the number of vertices and $c$ is the number of colors.

This theorem is particularly useful in the study of hypergraphs, which are generalizations of graphs where the edges can have multiple vertices. In fact, the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem can be extended to hypergraphs, providing a powerful tool for understanding the structure of these complex objects.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the proof of this theorem and its applications. We will also discuss some of its variations and explore how it relates to other important results in combinatorics.




#### 4.1b Proof and Derivations

The proof of the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines is a beautiful application of combinatorial reasoning and group theory. It is a direct consequence of the Cauchy-Frobenius lemma, which provides a formula for the number of fixed points of a group action.

#### 4.1b.1 Proof Sketch

The proof begins by considering the group of symmetries of the complete graph. This group, denoted by $S_n$, acts on the set of edges of the graph. The number of monochromatic lines is then given by the number of fixed points of this action.

Using the Cauchy-Frobenius lemma, we can express this number as the sum of the dimensions of the irreducible representations of $S_n$ that appear in the representation of the group of symmetries of the complete graph.

The group of symmetries of the complete graph is isomorphic to the symmetric group $S_n$, which has $n!$ elements. The representation of this group on the set of edges of the graph is the permutation representation, which is the direct sum of the trivial representation and the sign representation.

The trivial representation has dimension 1 and appears with multiplicity $n!/n = n$. The sign representation has dimension 1 and appears with multiplicity $(n!/n)(n!/2) = n(n-1)/2$.

Therefore, the number of monochromatic lines is given by:

$$
\text{Number of monochromatic lines} = n + \frac{n(n-1)}{2} = \frac{n^2 - n + 2}{2}
$$

This proves the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines.

#### 4.1b.2 Derivations

The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem can also be derived from the properties of the exterior calculus. The exterior calculus is a mathematical framework that provides a systematic way of constructing differential forms and their derivatives. It is particularly useful in the study of manifolds and differential geometry.

The proof begins by considering the exterior derivative of a differential form. The exterior derivative is a map that assigns to each differential form a new differential form of one higher degree. It satisfies several important properties, including the Leibniz rule, which states that the exterior derivative of a product of differential forms is the sum of the exterior derivatives of the individual forms.

The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem can be derived from these properties by considering the exterior derivative of a differential form that represents the coloring of the edges of the complete graph. The number of monochromatic lines is then given by the rank of this differential form, which is the minimum number of monomial terms that must be summed to produce it.

This derivation provides a new perspective on the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem, highlighting its connection to the properties of differential forms and the exterior calculus. It also provides a powerful tool for understanding the structure of the complete graph and the number of its monochromatic lines.

#### 4.1b.3 Generalizations

The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem can be generalized to hypergraphs, which are generalizations of graphs where the edges can have multiple vertices. The number of monochromatic lines in a hypergraph can be expressed in terms of the number of vertices and the number of colors, similar to the original theorem.

The proof of this generalization involves considering the group of symmetries of the hypergraph and applying the Cauchy-Frobenius lemma, similar to the proof of the original theorem. The key difference is that the group of symmetries of the hypergraph is now a subgroup of the symmetric group $S_n$, and the representation of this group on the set of edges of the hypergraph is now a direct sum of the trivial representation and the sign representation, with multiplicities that depend on the structure of the hypergraph.

This generalization provides a powerful tool for understanding the structure of hypergraphs and the number of their monochromatic lines. It also highlights the importance of group theory and representation theory in the study of combinatorial structures.

#### 4.1b.4 Applications

The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem has many applications in combinatorics and graph theory. One of the most important applications is in the study of hypergraphs. The number of monochromatic lines in a hypergraph can be used to determine the structure of the hypergraph and to solve various optimization problems.

The theorem also has applications in the study of colorings of graphs. The number of monochromatic lines can be used to determine the chromatic number of a graph, which is the minimum number of colors needed to color the vertices of the graph such that no two adjacent vertices have the same color.

Furthermore, the theorem has applications in the study of symmetric functions. The number of monochromatic lines can be used to determine the number of fixed points of a group action, which is a key concept in the study of symmetric functions.

In conclusion, the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines is a powerful tool in combinatorics and graph theory. Its proof and derivations provide a deep understanding of the structure of graphs and hypergraphs, and its applications have wide-ranging implications in various areas of mathematics.




#### 4.1c Applications and Examples

The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines has a wide range of applications in various fields, including graph theory, combinatorics, and computer science. In this section, we will explore some of these applications and provide examples to illustrate the theorem in action.

#### 4.1c.1 Graph Theory

In graph theory, the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem is used to study the structure of complete graphs. The theorem provides a formula for the number of monochromatic lines in a complete graph, which can be used to analyze the connectivity and colorability of the graph.

For example, consider a complete graph $K_n$ with $n$ vertices. The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem tells us that the number of monochromatic lines in $K_n$ is given by the formula:

$$
\text{Number of monochromatic lines} = \frac{n^2 - n + 2}{2}
$$

This formula can be used to determine the number of monochromatic lines in a complete graph of any size, providing a powerful tool for analyzing the structure of these graphs.

#### 4.1c.2 Combinatorics

In combinatorics, the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem is used to study the properties of monochromatic lines in general graphs. The theorem provides a formula for the number of monochromatic lines in a complete graph, which can be used to analyze the number of monochromatic lines in any graph.

For example, consider a graph $G$ with $n$ vertices. The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem tells us that the number of monochromatic lines in $G$ is at least as large as the number of monochromatic lines in the complete graph $K_n$. This provides a lower bound on the number of monochromatic lines in $G$, which can be useful in various combinatorial problems.

#### 4.1c.3 Computer Science

In computer science, the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem is used in the design and analysis of algorithms for various problems, including graph coloring and network design. The theorem provides a theoretical guarantee on the number of monochromatic lines in a complete graph, which can be used to design efficient algorithms for these problems.

For example, consider the problem of coloring the edges of a complete graph. The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem tells us that the number of monochromatic lines in a complete graph is at most $\frac{n^2 - n + 2}{2}$. This provides a theoretical upper bound on the number of edges that need to be colored in order to ensure that there are no monochromatic lines in the graph. This upper bound can be used to design an efficient algorithm for coloring the edges of a complete graph.

In conclusion, the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines is a powerful tool in geometric combinatorics, with applications in various fields. Its applications and examples provide a deeper understanding of the theorem and its implications, and serve as a foundation for further study in this fascinating field.

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of geometric combinatorics, specifically focusing on the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem. This theorem, named after the Hungarian mathematician Endre Szemerédi and the American mathematician Daniel Trotter, is a fundamental result in the field of combinatorial geometry. It provides a powerful tool for understanding the structure of high-dimensional geometric objects, and has found numerous applications in various areas of mathematics and computer science.

The Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem is a cornerstone of the study of geometric combinatorics. It provides a deep insight into the structure of high-dimensional geometric objects, and has found numerous applications in various areas of mathematics and computer science. The theorem is named after the Hungarian mathematician Endre Szemerédi and the American mathematician Daniel Trotter, who first proved it in the 1970s.

The theorem states that for any finite set of points in a high-dimensional Euclidean space, there exists a subspace of dimension at least $k$ that contains at most $N^{1/k}$ points. This result is particularly useful when dealing with large sets of points in high-dimensional spaces, as it allows us to control the complexity of the set by restricting our attention to a lower-dimensional subspace.

In this chapter, we will explore the proof of the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem, its implications, and its applications. We will also discuss some of the key ideas and techniques that are used in the proof, such as the concept of a "good" subspace and the use of the "Voronoi diagram" to control the complexity of the set. By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem and its role in geometric combinatorics.

#### 5.1b Proof and Derivations

The proof of the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem is a complex and intricate affair, involving a series of steps and derivations that build up to the final result. In this section, we will outline the key steps of the proof, and provide a detailed explanation of the derivations involved.

#### 5.1b.1 The Key Steps of the Proof

The proof of the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem can be broadly divided into three main steps:

1. **Reduction to a Lower-Dimensional Subspace:** The first step of the proof involves reducing the problem to a lower-dimensional subspace. This is achieved by considering a "good" subspace, which is a subspace of dimension $k$ that contains at most $N^{1/k}$ points. The existence of such a subspace is guaranteed by the theorem.

2. **Control of the Complexity of the Set:** Once we have reduced the problem to a lower-dimensional subspace, we can control the complexity of the set by restricting our attention to this subspace. This is achieved by using the concept of the Voronoi diagram, which provides a way to partition the points in the subspace into "cells" of bounded complexity.

3. **Application of the Voronoi Diagram:** The final step of the proof involves applying the Voronoi diagram to control the complexity of the set. This is achieved by showing that the Voronoi cells have bounded complexity, and hence the set has bounded complexity.

#### 5.1b.2 The Derivations Involved

The proof of the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem involves a series of derivations that build up to the final result. These derivations are based on a variety of mathematical tools and techniques, including the concept of a "good" subspace, the use of the Voronoi diagram, and the application of the Voronoi diagram to control the complexity of the set.

The proof begins with the assumption that the set of points in the high-dimensional Euclidean space is finite. This assumption leads to the existence of a "good" subspace, which is a subspace of dimension $k$ that contains at most $N^{1/k}$ points. This is achieved by considering the set of points in the subspace and using the concept of the Voronoi diagram to partition the points into "cells" of bounded complexity.

The proof then proceeds to show that the Voronoi cells have bounded complexity, and hence the set has bounded complexity. This is achieved by applying the Voronoi diagram to control the complexity of the set. The proof concludes with the final result, which is the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the details of the proof, providing a step-by-step explanation of the key steps and derivations involved.

#### 5.1c.2 Applications in Computer Science

The Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem also has important applications in computer science. One of the key areas is in the design and analysis of algorithms. The theorem provides a way to control the complexity of the set of points in the high-dimensional Euclidean space, which is crucial in designing efficient algorithms.

For example, consider the problem of finding the shortest path between two points in a graph. This problem can be formulated as a problem in the high-dimensional Euclidean space, and the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem can be used to control the complexity of the set of points in this space. This, in turn, can be used to design an efficient algorithm for finding the shortest path.

#### 5.1c.3 Examples in Mathematics

The Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem also has numerous examples in mathematics. One of the key examples is in the study of the Voronoi diagram. The theorem provides a way to control the complexity of the Voronoi diagram, which is crucial in many mathematical problems.

For example, consider the problem of finding the smallest circle that contains all the points in a given set. This problem can be formulated as a problem in the high-dimensional Euclidean space, and the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem can be used to control the complexity of the set of points in this space. This, in turn, can be used to find the smallest circle that contains all the points in the given set.

In conclusion, the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem is a powerful tool in geometric combinatorics, with wide-ranging applications in various fields. By understanding the theorem and its applications, we can gain a deeper understanding of the complexities of high-dimensional spaces and design more efficient algorithms and solutions to various problems.

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of geometric combinatorics, specifically focusing on unit distances, unit area triangles, Beck's Two Extremities Theorem, and the Weak Dirac Conjecture. These topics are fundamental to understanding the underlying principles of geometric combinatorics and have wide-ranging applications in various fields such as computer science, mathematics, and engineering.

We will begin by exploring the concept of unit distances, which is a fundamental concept in geometric combinatorics. Unit distances refer to the distances between points in a geometric space, where the distance between two points is normalized to be 1. Understanding unit distances is crucial for many geometric problems, as it allows us to simplify the problem and focus on the relative positions of points.

Next, we will delve into the concept of unit area triangles. A unit area triangle is a triangle with an area of 1. Unit area triangles are of particular interest in geometric combinatorics due to their connection with the famous Pythagorean theorem. We will explore this connection and its implications in more detail.

We will then move on to Beck's Two Extremities Theorem, which is a fundamental result in geometric combinatorics. This theorem provides a powerful tool for proving the existence of certain geometric configurations. We will explore the theorem in detail and discuss its applications.

Finally, we will discuss the Weak Dirac Conjecture, which is a famous open problem in geometric combinatorics. This conjecture is related to the concept of unit distances and has been a subject of intense study due to its implications for various geometric problems. We will discuss the current state of research on this conjecture and its potential implications.

Throughout this chapter, we will use the popular Markdown format to present the material, with math equations rendered using the MathJax library. This will allow us to present complex mathematical concepts in a clear and concise manner. We will also provide references to support any factual claims or opinions made in the text.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into each of these topics, providing a comprehensive study of geometric combinatorics. We hope that this chapter will serve as a valuable resource for anyone interested in understanding the fascinating world of geometric combinatorics.




### Subsection: 6.1a Definitions and Statements

#### 6.1a.1 Unit Distances

Unit distances are a fundamental concept in geometric combinatorics. They refer to the distances between points in a geometric space, where the distance between two points is normalized to be 1. This normalization allows us to simplify the problem and focus on the relative positions of points. 

#### 6.1a.2 Unit Area Triangles

A unit area triangle is a triangle with an area of 1. Unit area triangles are of particular interest in geometric combinatorics due to their connection with the famous Pythagorean theorem. The Pythagorean theorem states that in a right triangle, the square of the length of the hypotenuse is equal to the sum of the squares of the lengths of the other two sides. This theorem is fundamental to the study of unit area triangles, as it allows us to determine the lengths of the sides of a unit area triangle.

#### 6.1a.3 Beck's Two Extremities Theorem

Beck's Two Extremities Theorem is a fundamental result in geometric combinatorics. It provides a powerful tool for proving the existence of certain geometric configurations. The theorem states that given a set of points in the plane, if the distance between any two points is at least 1, then there exists a triangle with vertices in this set of points. This theorem has wide-ranging applications in various fields, including computer science and engineering.

#### 6.1a.4 Weak Dirac Conjecture

The Weak Dirac Conjecture is a famous open problem in geometric combinatorics. It is related to the concept of unit distances and has been a subject of intense study due to its implications for various geometric problems. The conjecture states that given a set of points in the plane, if the distance between any two points is at least 1, then there exists a triangle with vertices in this set of points. This conjecture is a weaker version of the Strong Dirac Conjecture, which states that given a set of points in the plane, if the distance between any two points is at least 1, then there exists a triangle with vertices in this set of points such that the distance between any two vertices is at least 1. The Weak Dirac Conjecture is a fundamental problem in geometric combinatorics, and its solution would have significant implications for the field.




### Subsection: 6.1b Proofs and Derivations

#### 6.1b.1 Proof of Beck's Two Extremities Theorem

The proof of Beck's Two Extremities Theorem is a beautiful application of the Pythagorean theorem and the concept of unit distances. 

Given a set of points in the plane, if the distance between any two points is at least 1, then there exists a triangle with vertices in this set of points.

Proof:

Let's consider a set of points $P = \{p_1, p_2, ..., p_n\}$ in the plane such that the distance between any two points is at least 1. We will prove that there exists a triangle with vertices in this set of points.

Let's construct a unit area triangle $T = \{p_1, p_2, p_3\}$ from the points in $P$. We will show that this triangle exists by proving that the area of $T$ is 1.

By the Pythagorean theorem, the square of the length of the hypotenuse of a right triangle is equal to the sum of the squares of the lengths of the other two sides. In our case, the hypotenuse of $T$ is the line segment connecting $p_1$ and $p_3$, and the other two sides are the line segments connecting $p_1$ and $p_2$, and $p_2$ and $p_3$.

Let's denote the length of these line segments as $a$, $b$, and $c$, respectively. By the Pythagorean theorem, we have $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$.

The area of $T$ is given by the formula $\frac{1}{2} \times a \times b = \frac{1}{2} \times \sqrt{c^2 - b^2} \times b$.

Substituting $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$ into this formula, we get $\frac{1}{2} \times \sqrt{c^2 - b^2} \times b = \frac{1}{2} \times \sqrt{c^2 - (c^2 - a^2)} \times b = \frac{1}{2} \times \sqrt{a^2} \times b = \frac{1}{2} \times b \times b = \frac{1}{2} \times b^2$.

Since the area of $T$ is $\frac{1}{2} \times b^2$, and the area of a unit area triangle is 1, we have $\frac{1}{2} \times b^2 = 1$. Therefore, $b = \sqrt{2}$.

Since the distance between any two points in $P$ is at least 1, the length of the line segment connecting $p_1$ and $p_2$ is at least 1. Therefore, $b \geq 1$.

Since $b = \sqrt{2}$, we have $b^2 = 2$. Therefore, the area of $T$ is $\frac{1}{2} \times b^2 = \frac{1}{2} \times 2 = 1$.

Therefore, there exists a triangle with vertices in $P$, and the area of this triangle is 1. This proves Beck's Two Extremities Theorem.

#### 6.1b.2 Derivation of the Weak Dirac Conjecture

The Weak Dirac Conjecture is a powerful tool in the study of unit distances and unit area triangles. It provides a way to determine the existence of a triangle with vertices in a given set of points, based on the concept of unit distances.

The Weak Dirac Conjecture states that given a set of points $P = \{p_1, p_2, ..., p_n\}$ in the plane, if the distance between any two points is at least 1, then there exists a triangle with vertices in this set of points.

Proof:

The proof of the Weak Dirac Conjecture is a direct consequence of Beck's Two Extremities Theorem. 

Let's consider a set of points $P = \{p_1, p_2, ..., p_n\}$ in the plane such that the distance between any two points is at least 1. We will prove that there exists a triangle with vertices in this set of points.

By Beck's Two Extremities Theorem, there exists a triangle $T = \{p_1, p_2, p_3\}$ with vertices in $P$. We will show that this triangle exists by proving that the area of $T$ is 1.

The area of $T$ is given by the formula $\frac{1}{2} \times a \times b = \frac{1}{2} \times \sqrt{c^2 - b^2} \times b$, where $a$, $b$, and $c$ are the lengths of the line segments connecting the vertices of $T$.

By the Pythagorean theorem, we have $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$. Therefore, the area of $T$ is $\frac{1}{2} \times \sqrt{c^2 - b^2} \times b = \frac{1}{2} \times \sqrt{c^2 - (c^2 - a^2)} \times b = \frac{1}{2} \times \sqrt{a^2} \times b = \frac{1}{2} \times b^2$.

Since the area of $T$ is $\frac{1}{2} \times b^2$, and the area of a unit area triangle is 1, we have $\frac{1}{2} \times b^2 = 1$. Therefore, $b = \sqrt{2}$.

Since the distance between any two points in $P$ is at least 1, the length of the line segment connecting $p_1$ and $p_2$ is at least 1. Therefore, $b \geq 1$.

Since $b = \sqrt{2}$, we have $b^2 = 2$. Therefore, the area of $T$ is $\frac{1}{2} \times b^2 = \frac{1}{2} \times 2 = 1$.

Therefore, there exists a triangle with vertices in $P$, and the area of this triangle is 1. This proves the Weak Dirac Conjecture.

### Subsection: 6.2a Lemma Statement

The Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs is a fundamental result in the study of geometric combinatorics. It provides a powerful tool for analyzing the structure of multigraphs, which are graphs that allow multiple edges between the same pair of vertices. The lemma is particularly useful in the study of unit distances and unit area triangles, as it allows us to understand the structure of these objects in terms of multigraphs.

#### 6.2a.1 Statement of the Lemma

The Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs can be stated as follows:

Given a multigraph $G$, let $C$ be a cycle in $G$. If $C$ contains $k$ edges, then $C$ contains at least $k$ vertices.

This lemma is a simple but powerful tool for analyzing the structure of multigraphs. It allows us to prove important results about the structure of multigraphs, such as the fact that every multigraph contains a cycle of even length.

#### 6.2a.2 Applications of the Crossing Lemma

The Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs has a wide range of applications in the study of geometric combinatorics. One of the most important applications is in the study of unit distances and unit area triangles.

For instance, consider the 6mm PPC, a project currently in progress. The 6mm PPC is a variant of the PPC (PowerPC) architecture that uses unit area triangles in its design. The Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs can be used to analyze the structure of the 6mm PPC, providing insights into its properties and behavior.

Another important application of the Crossing Lemma is in the study of the Weak Dirac Conjecture. This conjecture, which is a generalization of the Dirac Conjecture, is a fundamental result in the study of unit distances and unit area triangles. The Crossing Lemma can be used to prove important results about the Weak Dirac Conjecture, providing a deeper understanding of its properties and implications.

In the next section, we will explore these applications in more detail, and discuss how the Crossing Lemma can be used to prove important results about unit distances, unit area triangles, and the Weak Dirac Conjecture.

### Subsection: 6.2b Proof of Lemma

The proof of the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs is a straightforward application of the definition of a multigraph and the properties of cycles. We will prove the lemma by contradiction, assuming that the cycle $C$ contains $k$ edges but only $k-1$ vertices.

#### 6.2b.1 Contradiction Assumption

Let $C$ be a cycle in the multigraph $G$ containing $k$ edges. We assume that $C$ contains only $k-1$ vertices. This assumption leads to a contradiction, which we will now derive.

#### 6.2b.2 Deriving the Contradiction

Consider the cycle $C$ in the multigraph $G$. Since $C$ contains $k$ edges, it must contain at least one edge that is repeated. Let $e$ be such an edge.

Now, consider the cycle $C'$ obtained from $C$ by removing the edge $e$. Since $C'$ contains $k-1$ edges, it must contain at least one vertex that is repeated. Let $v$ be such a vertex.

But $v$ cannot be repeated in $C'$, since $C'$ contains only $k-1$ vertices. This leads to a contradiction, since $v$ is repeated in $C'$.

#### 6.2b.3 Conclusion

We have reached a contradiction, which proves the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs. This lemma is a powerful tool for analyzing the structure of multigraphs, and it has many applications in the study of geometric combinatorics. In the next section, we will explore some of these applications in more detail.

### Subsection: 6.2c Applications and Examples

The Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs has a wide range of applications in the study of geometric combinatorics. In this section, we will explore some of these applications and provide examples to illustrate the lemma in action.

#### 6.2c.1 Applications of the Crossing Lemma

One of the most important applications of the Crossing Lemma is in the study of unit distances and unit area triangles. These objects are fundamental to many areas of combinatorics, including graph theory, topology, and computational geometry.

For instance, consider the 6mm PPC, a project currently in progress. The 6mm PPC is a variant of the PPC (PowerPC) architecture that uses unit area triangles in its design. The Crossing Lemma can be used to analyze the structure of the 6mm PPC, providing insights into its properties and behavior.

Another important application of the Crossing Lemma is in the study of the Weak Dirac Conjecture. This conjecture, which is a generalization of the Dirac Conjecture, is a fundamental result in the study of unit distances and unit area triangles. The Crossing Lemma can be used to prove important results about the Weak Dirac Conjecture, providing a deeper understanding of its properties and implications.

#### 6.2c.2 Examples of the Crossing Lemma

To further illustrate the Crossing Lemma, let's consider a simple example. Consider a multigraph $G$ with a cycle $C$ containing $k$ edges. If we remove an edge from $C$, the resulting cycle $C'$ will contain $k-1$ edges. By the Crossing Lemma, $C'$ must contain at least $k-1$ vertices. This shows that the removal of an edge from a cycle in a multigraph cannot decrease the number of vertices in the cycle.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the study of unit distances and unit area triangles, and explore more applications and examples of the Crossing Lemma.

## Chapter: Chapter 7: Theta Graphs, Theta Bounds, and Theta Function

### Introduction

In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of Theta Graphs, Theta Bounds, and Theta Function, three fundamental concepts in the field of geometric combinatorics. These concepts are not only of theoretical interest but also have practical applications in various areas such as computer science, mathematics, and engineering.

Theta Graphs, also known as theta grids, are a type of graph that are particularly useful in the study of combinatorial geometry. They are defined as the intersection of two parabolas, and their study has led to significant insights into the structure of geometric objects. Theta Graphs have been used in a variety of applications, from the design of efficient algorithms to the study of geometric transformations.

Theta Bounds, on the other hand, are a set of inequalities that provide a way to bound the number of vertices, edges, and faces in a geometric object. These bounds are particularly useful in the study of polyhedra and other geometric objects. Theta Bounds have been instrumental in the development of efficient algorithms for the construction and manipulation of geometric objects.

Finally, the Theta Function, also known as the Jacobi Theta Function, is a mathematical function that has been studied extensively in the field of number theory and combinatorics. The Theta Function plays a crucial role in the study of Theta Graphs and Theta Bounds, and its properties have been used to derive many important results in geometric combinatorics.

Throughout this chapter, we will explore these concepts in depth, providing a comprehensive study of their properties, applications, and implications. We will also discuss the interplay between these concepts, and how they form a cohesive framework for the study of geometric combinatorics. By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of Theta Graphs, Theta Bounds, and Theta Function, and be equipped with the knowledge to apply these concepts in your own research or practice.




#### 6.2b Proof and Derivations

The proof of the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs is a straightforward application of the pigeonhole principle. We will provide a detailed proof below.

#### 6.2b.1 Proof of the Crossing Lemma

Given a multigraph $G$ and a cycle $C$ in $G$, let $V$ be the set of vertices in $C$ and $E$ be the set of edges in $C$. Since $C$ is a cycle, every vertex in $V$ has degree 2. 

We will prove the lemma by contradiction. Suppose that $C$ contains $k$ edges but only $k-1$ vertices. This means that there exists a vertex $v \in V$ that is incident to two edges in $C$. 

Let $e_1$ and $e_2$ be the two edges incident to $v$. Since $e_1$ and $e_2$ are in $C$, they must have a vertex in common other than $v$. Let $w$ be this vertex. 

Since $w$ is incident to two edges in $C$, it must be incident to at least two other edges in $C$. This means that there exists a path in $C$ from $w$ to $v$ that does not contain any of the edges $e_1$ or $e_2$. This path must contain at least two edges, since $w$ is incident to at least two edges in $C$. 

This contradicts the assumption that $C$ contains only $k-1$ vertices. Therefore, $C$ must contain at least $k$ vertices. 

#### 6.2b.2 Derivations of the Crossing Lemma

The Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs has several important derivations that provide deeper insights into the structure of multigraphs. 

One of these derivations is the Weak Dirac Conjecture, which states that every multigraph contains a cycle of even length. This conjecture is a direct consequence of the Crossing Lemma. 

Another important derivation is the Crossing Lemma for Unit Distances, which states that every multigraph contains a cycle of even length. This lemma is a special case of the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs, and it is particularly useful in the study of unit distances and unit area triangles.

The Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs also has implications for the study of the 6mm PPC, a project currently in progress. The 6mm PPC is a variant of the PPC (PowerPC) architecture that uses unit area triangles in its design. The Crossing Lemma can be used to analyze the structure of the 6mm PPC, providing insights into its properties and behavior.

In the next section, we will explore these applications in more detail, and discuss how the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs can be used to prove important results about the Weak Dirac Conjecture, the Crossing Lemma for Unit Distances, and the 6mm PPC.

#### 6.2c Applications and Examples

The Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs has a wide range of applications in the study of geometric combinatorics. In this section, we will explore some of these applications and provide examples to illustrate the lemma in action.

##### 6.2c.1 Applications of the Crossing Lemma

One of the most significant applications of the Crossing Lemma is in the study of the Weak Dirac Conjecture. As mentioned in the previous section, the Weak Dirac Conjecture is a direct consequence of the Crossing Lemma. This conjecture has been a subject of study for many years, and its proof has been a major goal in the field of geometric combinatorics. ##### 6.2c.2 Examples of the Crossing Lemma

To illustrate the Crossing Lemma in action, let's consider a simple example. Consider a multigraph $G$ with a cycle $C$ that contains 5 edges. By the Crossing Lemma, we know that $C$ must contain at least 5 vertices. This can be seen by considering the pigeonhole principle. Since there are only 5 edges in $C$, and each edge is incident to two vertices, there must be at least 5 vertices in $C$.

This example illustrates the power of the Crossing Lemma. It allows us to make a simple but powerful statement about the structure of multigraphs, and it provides a tool for proving important results about the Weak Dirac Conjecture, unit distances, and the 6mm PPC.

## Chapter 7: Theta Functions

### Introduction

In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of Theta Functions, a fundamental concept in the study of geometric combinatorics. Theta functions, named after the Greek letter 'θ' (theta), are mathematical functions that have been studied extensively in number theory, algebraic geometry, and statistical mechanics. They are particularly important in the study of elliptic curves and modular forms.

Theta functions are defined as theta series, which are infinite series that involve the argument 'z' and the variables 'q' and 't'. Theta functions are defined for complex values of 'z', but they are often studied for 'z' in the upper half-plane, where they have particularly nice properties.

The study of theta functions is a rich and rewarding field, with many applications in various areas of mathematics. In this chapter, we will explore the basic properties of theta functions, their connection with elliptic curves, and their role in the study of geometric combinatorics.

We will begin by introducing the basic definition of theta functions and theta series. We will then explore the properties of theta functions, including their periodicity, their behavior under transformation of the argument, and their connection with the Jacobi theta function. We will also discuss the role of theta functions in the study of elliptic curves and modular forms.

Finally, we will discuss the applications of theta functions in geometric combinatorics. This includes their role in the study of unit distances and unit area triangles, as well as their connection with the Weak Dirac Conjecture.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of theta functions and their role in the study of geometric combinatorics. You will also have the tools to explore further the fascinating world of theta functions and their applications.

## Chapter: Chapter 7: Theta Graphs, Theta Bounds, Theta Function

In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of Theta Graphs, Theta Bounds, and Theta Function, three fundamental concepts in the field of geometric combinatorics. These concepts are not only mathematically intriguing but also have wide-ranging applications in various fields such as computer science, engineering, and physics.

Theta Graphs, also known as theta grids, are a type of graph that are particularly useful in the study of combinatorial geometry. They are defined as the intersection of two infinite families of lines, and they have been extensively studied due to their unique properties. Theta graphs have been used in a variety of applications, from coding theory to graph theory.

Theta Bounds, on the other hand, are mathematical inequalities that provide upper and lower bounds on the number of vertices in a theta graph. These bounds are crucial in the study of theta graphs, as they help us understand the structure and properties of these graphs. Theta bounds have been instrumental in the development of various algorithms and techniques in combinatorial geometry.

Lastly, the Theta Function, also known as the Jacobi Theta Function, is a mathematical function that plays a crucial role in the study of theta graphs and theta bounds. It is defined as a series and has been extensively studied due to its properties and applications. The Theta Function has been used in a variety of fields, from number theory to statistical mechanics.

### Subsection: 6.3a Problem Statement

In this section, we will introduce the problem of determining the minimum number of distinct distances in a set of points in the plane. This problem is a fundamental question in geometric combinatorics, with applications in various fields such as computer science, mathematics, and engineering.

#### 6.3a.1 The Problem

Given a set of $n$ points in the plane, the problem is to determine the minimum number of distinct distances between these points. In other words, we are interested in finding the smallest number of different distances that can be formed by pairs of points in the set.

#### 6.3a.2 Examples

Consider the set of points $\{A, B, C, D\}$ in the plane, where $A$ is the origin, $B$ is at distance 1 from $A$, $C$ is at distance 2 from $A$, and $D$ is at distance 3 from $A$. In this case, the minimum number of distinct distances is 3, since we have the distances $AB = 1$, $AC = 2$, and $AD = 3$.

On the other hand, if we consider the set of points $\{A, B, C, D\}$ where $A$ is the origin, $B$ is at distance 1 from $A$, $C$ is at distance 2 from $A$, and $D$ is at distance 2 from $A$, the minimum number of distinct distances is 2, since we only have the distances $AB = 1$ and $AC = 2$.

#### 6.3a.3 Applications

The problem of determining the minimum number of distinct distances has various applications in different fields. In computer science, it is used in the design and analysis of algorithms for data compression, where the goal is to reduce the amount of data needed to represent a set of points.

In mathematics, the problem is used in the study of various mathematical structures, such as groups, rings, and fields. For instance, it is used in the study of permutation groups, where it is used to prove that certain permutations cannot be expressed as a product of other permutations.

In engineering, the problem is used in the design and analysis of various systems, such as communication systems, power systems, and transportation systems. For instance, it is used in the design of communication systems, where it is used to ensure that the communication signals do not interfere with each other.

In the next section, we will explore some techniques for solving this problem.




### Subsection: 6.3b Solutions and Approaches

In this section, we will explore various solutions and approaches to the problem of determining the minimum number of distinct distances in a set of points in the plane.

#### 6.3b.1 Brute Force Approach

The brute force approach to this problem involves checking all possible pairs of points in the set and determining the number of distinct distances between them. This approach is simple but not very efficient, as the number of pairs of points is $O(n^2)$.

#### 6.3b.2 Dynamic Programming Approach

The dynamic programming approach is a more efficient way to solve this problem. It involves breaking down the problem into smaller subproblems and storing the solutions to these subproblems in a table. The solution to the original problem can then be obtained by combining the solutions to the subproblems. This approach is more complex than the brute force approach, but it is also more efficient, with a time complexity of $O(n^2)$.

#### 6.3b.3 Geometric Approach

The geometric approach involves using geometric properties to determine the minimum number of distinct distances. For example, if all the points in the set lie on a circle, the minimum number of distinct distances is equal to the number of points minus one. This approach is more intuitive than the previous two approaches, but it may not always work for all sets of points.

#### 6.3b.4 Algorithmic Approach

The algorithmic approach involves designing an algorithm that can efficiently compute the minimum number of distinct distances. This approach combines elements of the previous three approaches and may involve the use of data structures and other techniques.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into each of these approaches and discuss their advantages and disadvantages.

In combinatorics, the minimum number of distinct distances can be used to determine the structure of a graph. This can be useful in various combinatorial problems, such as determining the number of vertices in a connected component or finding the shortest path between two vertices.

#### 6.3c.3 Implications in Computer Science

In computer science, the minimum number of distinct distances can be used in various algorithms and data structures. ## Chapter: Chapter 7: Theta-Graphs and Theta-Series

### Introduction

In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of Theta-Graphs and Theta-Series, two fundamental concepts in the field of geometric combinatorics. These concepts are not only mathematically intriguing but also have wide-ranging applications in various fields such as computer science, physics, and engineering.

Theta-Graphs, also known as theta graphs or theta-grids, are a type of graph that are used to represent certain types of combinatorial structures. They are particularly useful in the study of symmetric functions, which are functions that are invariant under certain symmetries. Theta-Graphs provide a visual representation of these symmetries, making them a powerful tool for understanding and analyzing symmetric functions.

On the other hand, Theta-Series are mathematical series that are used to represent certain types of functions. They are particularly useful in the study of modular forms, which are functions that are invariant under certain transformations. Theta-Series provide a way to express these functions in a compact and elegant manner, making them a powerful tool for understanding and analyzing modular forms.

In this chapter, we will explore the properties and applications of Theta-Graphs and Theta-Series. We will start by introducing the basic concepts and definitions, and then move on to more advanced topics such as theta-graphs of symmetric functions and theta-series of modular forms. We will also discuss some of the key results and techniques in these areas, such as the Jacobi theta function and the Ramanujan theta function.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of Theta-Graphs and Theta-Series, and be able to apply these concepts to solve problems in geometric combinatorics. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in a related field, this chapter will provide you with the knowledge and tools you need to explore and understand these fascinating concepts.




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of geometric combinatorics, specifically focusing on unit distances, unit area triangles, Beck’s Two Extremities Theorem, and the Weak Dirac Conjecture. We have delved into the intricacies of these concepts, understanding their significance and applications in various fields.

We began by discussing unit distances, a fundamental concept in geometric combinatorics. We learned that unit distances are the shortest distances between two points in a given space. This concept is crucial in many areas, including computer graphics, where it is used to determine the closest points in a scene.

Next, we moved on to unit area triangles, another fundamental concept in geometric combinatorics. We learned that unit area triangles are triangles with an area of 1. These triangles are particularly important in the study of convex polyhedra, where they are used to construct the vertices of the polyhedron.

We then explored Beck's Two Extremities Theorem, a powerful result in geometric combinatorics. This theorem provides a necessary and sufficient condition for a set of points to be the vertices of a convex polyhedron. It is a fundamental tool in the study of convex polyhedra and has numerous applications in various fields, including computer graphics and combinatorial optimization.

Finally, we delved into the Weak Dirac Conjecture, a conjecture in geometric combinatorics that has been a subject of intense study for many years. This conjecture provides a lower bound on the number of vertices of a convex polyhedron. While it has been proven for certain classes of polyhedra, it remains an open problem for general polyhedra.

In conclusion, this chapter has provided a comprehensive study of these fundamental concepts in geometric combinatorics. We have seen how these concepts are interconnected and how they are used in various fields. The study of geometric combinatorics is a vast and exciting field, and we hope that this chapter has sparked your interest and curiosity to explore further.

Next, we moved on to unit area triangles, another fundamental concept in geometric combinatorics. We learned that unit area triangles are triangles with an area of 1. These triangles are particularly important in the study of convex polyhedra, where they are used to construct the vertices of the polyhedron.

We then explored Beck's Two Extremities Theorem, a powerful result in geometric combinatorics. This theorem provides a necessary and sufficient condition for a set of points to be the vertices of a convex polyhedron. It is a fundamental tool in the study of convex polyhedra and has numerous applications in various fields, including computer graphics and combinatorial optimization.

Finally, we delved into the Weak Dirac Conjecture, a conjecture in geometric combinatorics that has been a subject of intense study for many years. This conjecture provides a lower bound on the number of vertices of a convex polyhedron. While it has been proven for certain classes of polyhedra, it remains an open problem for general polyhedra.

In conclusion, this chapter has provided a comprehensive study of these fundamental concepts in geometric combinatorics. We have seen how these concepts are interconnected and how they are used in various fields. The study of geometric combinatorics is a vast and exciting field, and we hope that this chapter has sparked your interest and curiosity to explore further.

This theorem, named after its discoverers, Miklos Pach and Shimon Sharir, is a fundamental result in the field of combinatorial geometry. It provides a powerful tool for understanding the incidences between points and combinatorial curves, and has found numerous applications in various areas of mathematics.

The Pach-Sharir Theorem is a statement about the number of incidences between points and combinatorial curves in a finite set of points and curves. An incidence occurs when a point lies on a curve. The theorem provides an upper bound on the number of incidences, which is crucial in many applications. It also provides a way to construct a set of points and curves that achieves this upper bound.

This chapter will be divided into several sections, each focusing on a different aspect of the Pach-Sharir Theorem. ### Section: 7.1 Introduction to Pach-Sharir Theorem on Incidences of Points and Combinatorial Curves

The Pach-Sharir Theorem on Incidences of Points and Combinatorial Curves is a fundamental result in the field of combinatorial geometry. It provides a powerful tool for understanding the incidences between points and combinatorial curves, and has found numerous applications in various areas of mathematics.

#### 7.1a Definition and Statement

The Pach-Sharir Theorem can be stated as follows:

Given a set of $n$ points in the plane and a set of $m$ combinatorial curves, the number of incidences between these points and curves is at most $O(n^{2/3}m^{2/3})$.

Here, an incidence occurs when a point lies on a curve. The theorem provides an upper bound on the number of incidences, which is crucial in many applications. It also provides a way to construct a set of points and curves that achieves this upper bound.

The proof of the Pach-Sharir Theorem involves a careful analysis of the geometry of the incidences, combined with a combinatorial argument. It also relies on the concept of a "good" curve, which is a curve that does not contain too many points. The theorem shows that there are not too many good curves, which leads to the desired upper bound on the number of incidences.

The Pach-Sharir Theorem has numerous applications in various areas of mathematics. For example, it is used in the study of convexity, where it provides a way to understand the structure of convex sets. It is also used in computational geometry, where it is used to solve various problems involving points and curves.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the Pach-Sharir Theorem, exploring its key ideas, its proof, and its applications. We will also discuss some of the extensions and generalizations of the theorem, including its versions for higher dimensions and its applications in other areas of mathematics.

#### 7.1b Proof of Pach-Sharir Theorem

The proof of the Pach-Sharir Theorem is a beautiful blend of geometric intuition and combinatorial arguments. It is based on the concept of a "good" curve, which is a curve that does not contain too many points. The theorem shows that there are not too many good curves, which leads to the desired upper bound on the number of incidences.

Let's start by defining some key terms. A curve is said to be "good" if it contains at most $O(n^{2/3})$ points. A set of curves is said to be "good" if it contains at most $O(n^{2/3})$ good curves. The Pach-Sharir Theorem states that the number of incidences between a set of points and a set of good curves is at most $O(n^{2/3}m^{2/3})$.

The proof of the theorem proceeds in several steps. First, we show that there are not too many good curves. This is done by considering the number of incidences between the points and the curves. If there are too many incidences, then there must be a curve that contains too many points, which contradicts the definition of a good curve.

Next, we show that there are not too many good points. This is done by considering the number of incidences between the points and the curves. If there are too many incidences, then there must be a point that lies on too many curves, which contradicts the definition of a good point.

Finally, we show that the number of incidences between the points and the curves is at most $O(n^{2/3}m^{2/3})$. This is done by considering the number of incidences between the points and the curves. If there are too many incidences, then there must be a point that lies on too many curves, which contradicts the definition of a good point.

The proof of the Pach-Sharir Theorem is a powerful tool for understanding the incidences between points and combinatorial curves. It provides a way to construct a set of points and curves that achieves the upper bound on the number of incidences, which is crucial in many applications. In the next section, we will explore some of these applications in more detail.

#### 7.1c Applications of Pach-Sharir Theorem

The Pach-Sharir Theorem has found numerous applications in various areas of mathematics. In this section, we will explore some of these applications, focusing on their implications for combinatorial curves and incidences.

##### Combinatorial Curves

The Pach-Sharir Theorem has been instrumental in the study of combinatorial curves. It provides a powerful tool for understanding the structure of these curves and their relationship with points. For instance, it has been used to prove theorems about the number of points on a combinatorial curve, the number of combinatorial curves passing through a given point, and the number of incidences between points and combinatorial curves.

One of the key applications of the Pach-Sharir Theorem in the study of combinatorial curves is its role in the proof of the Erdős-Szekeres Theorem. This theorem states that for any $k \geq 3$, there exists a positive integer $n$ such that any set of $n$ points in the plane contains either $k$ points that are all on the same line, or $k$ points that are all on the same circle. The Pach-Sharir Theorem is used to prove this theorem by showing that there are not too many good curves in the set of points, which leads to a contradiction if the set of points does not contain $k$ points on the same line or circle.

##### Incidences

The Pach-Sharir Theorem also has important implications for the study of incidences between points and combinatorial curves. It provides a way to understand the structure of these incidences and their relationship with the points and curves. For instance, it has been used to prove theorems about the number of incidences between points and combinatorial curves, the number of incidences between combinatorial curves, and the number of incidences between points and lines.

One of the key applications of the Pach-Sharir Theorem in the study of incidences is its role in the proof of the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem. This theorem states that for any $k \geq 3$, there exists a positive integer $n$ such that any set of $n$ points in the plane contains either $k$ points that are all on the same line, or $k$ points that are all on the same circle. The Pach-Sharir Theorem is used to prove this theorem by showing that there are not too many good curves in the set of points, which leads to a contradiction if the set of points does not contain $k$ points on the same line or circle.

In conclusion, the Pach-Sharir Theorem is a powerful tool for understanding the structure of combinatorial curves and incidences. Its applications in various areas of mathematics have led to significant advancements in our understanding of these concepts.




#### 7.1b Proof and Derivations

The proof of the Pach-Sharir Theorem involves a careful analysis of the geometry of the incidences, combined with a combinatorial argument. It also relies on the concept of a "good" curve, which is a curve that does not contain too many points. The theorem shows that there are not too many good curves, which leads to the desired upper bound on the number of incidences.

#### Proof of the Pach-Sharir Theorem

The proof of the Pach-Sharir Theorem begins with the observation that every point lies on at most $m$ curves, and every curve contains at most $n$ points. This leads to the following upper bound on the number of incidences:

$$
I \leq nm
$$

The goal is to improve this upper bound. To this end, we introduce the concept of a "good" curve. A curve is said to be good if it contains at most $\sqrt{n}$ points. The theorem shows that there are not too many good curves, which leads to the desired upper bound on the number of incidences.

To prove this, we first observe that every point lies on at most $\sqrt{n}$ good curves. This is because every point lies on at most $m$ curves, and each of these curves contains at most $\sqrt{n}$ points. Therefore, the total number of incidences with good curves is at most $n\sqrt{n}$.

Next, we observe that every good curve contains at most $n$ points. This is because every good curve contains at most $\sqrt{n}$ points, and each of these points lies on at most $m$ curves. Therefore, the total number of incidences with good curves is at most $n\sqrt{n}$.

Combining these two observations, we find that the total number of incidences is at most $n\sqrt{n} + nm$. This is the desired upper bound, which improves the initial upper bound of $nm$.

The proof of the Pach-Sharir Theorem is complete. We have shown that the number of incidences between a set of points and a set of combinatorial curves is at most $O(n^{2/3}m^{2/3})$. This theorem has numerous applications in various areas of mathematics, including convexity and computational geometry.

#### Derivations of the Pach-Sharir Theorem

The Pach-Sharir Theorem can be derived from several key properties of combinatorial curves and points. These properties include the following:

1. Every point lies on at most $m$ curves.
2. Every curve contains at most $n$ points.
3. There are not too many good curves.
4. Every point lies on at most $\sqrt{n}$ good curves.
5. Every good curve contains at most $n$ points.

These properties lead to the desired upper bound on the number of incidences. The proof of the Pach-Sharir Theorem involves a careful analysis of these properties and their implications.

#### 7.2a Definitions and Concepts

In this section, we will introduce some key concepts and definitions that are essential for understanding the various crossing numbers and their applications.

#### Crossing Numbers

A crossing number is a measure of the complexity of a geometric object. It is defined as the maximum number of incidences between points and combinatorial curves in the object. The Pach-Sharir Theorem provides an upper bound on the crossing number of a set of points and combinatorial curves.

#### Combinatorial Curves

Combinatorial curves are a generalization of the concept of a curve in the plane. They are defined as the intersection of two half-planes. Each combinatorial curve divides the plane into two regions, and each region is bounded by a curve. The Pach-Sharir Theorem applies to combinatorial curves, not just to ordinary curves.

#### Incidences

An incidence is a pair of points and curves such that the points lie on the curves. The number of incidences between a set of points and a set of curves is a key factor in determining the complexity of the object. The Pach-Sharir Theorem provides an upper bound on the number of incidences.

#### Applications of Crossing Numbers

Crossing numbers have a wide range of applications in various areas of mathematics. They are used in the design and analysis of algorithms, in the study of geometric combinatorics, and in many other areas. The Pach-Sharir Theorem, with its upper bound on the crossing number, is a powerful tool for understanding and controlling the complexity of geometric objects.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the various types of crossing numbers and their properties. We will also explore some specific examples to illustrate these concepts in action.

#### 7.2b Properties and Theorems

In this section, we will explore some key properties and theorems related to crossing numbers. These properties and theorems will provide a deeper understanding of the concepts introduced in the previous section and their applications.

#### Properties of Crossing Numbers

1. **Monotonicity:** The crossing number of a set of points and combinatorial curves is monotone increasing in the number of points and curves. This means that adding more points or curves to the set can only increase the crossing number.

2. **Subset Property:** If a set of points and combinatorial curves has a crossing number of $k$, then any subset of this set has a crossing number at most $k$. This property is a direct consequence of the monotonicity property.

3. **Symmetry:** The crossing number of a set of points and combinatorial curves is symmetric. This means that if the set of points and curves is replaced by its complement, the crossing number remains the same.

#### Theorems Related to Crossing Numbers

1. **Pach-Sharir Theorem:** The Pach-Sharir Theorem provides an upper bound on the crossing number of a set of points and combinatorial curves. This theorem is a fundamental result in geometric combinatorics and has many applications.

2. **Khinchine-Tammes Theorem:** The Khinchine-Tammes Theorem provides a lower bound on the crossing number of a set of points and combinatorial curves. This theorem is a dual of the Pach-Sharir Theorem and is also a key result in geometric combinatorics.

3. **Folk Theorem:** The Folk Theorem is a conjecture that the crossing number of a set of points and combinatorial curves is equal to the number of incidences between these points and curves. This theorem is still open, but it has been verified for many special cases.

In the next section, we will explore some specific examples to illustrate these concepts in action. We will also discuss some applications of crossing numbers in various areas of mathematics.

2. **Combinatorial Geometry:** Crossing numbers are fundamental to the study of combinatorial geometry. For example, the Khinchine-Tammes Theorem provides a lower bound on the crossing number, which can be used to prove theorems about the existence of certain geometric objects.

3. **Computer Graphics:** Crossing numbers are also used in computer graphics to measure the complexity of scenes. The complexity of a scene can be used to determine the computational resources required to render the scene, which is crucial in real-time applications.

#### Examples of Crossing Numbers

1. **Line Integral Convolution:** Line Integral Convolution (LIC) is a technique used in computer graphics to render vector fields. The complexity of the vector field can be measured using the crossing number, which can then be used to determine the computational resources required to render the field.

## Chapter: Chapter 8: Theta Functions and Their Applications

### Introduction

In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of Theta Functions and their applications. Theta functions, named after the Greek letter 'theta', are mathematical functions that have been studied extensively in number theory, combinatorics, and statistical mechanics. They are particularly important in the study of elliptic curves and modular forms, and have found applications in a wide range of areas, from cryptography to statistical physics.

The chapter begins by introducing the basic concepts of Theta Functions, including their definition, properties, and the different types of Theta Functions. We will explore the Jacobi Theta Function, the Riemann Theta Function, and the Eisenstein Theta Function, among others. Each type of Theta Function has its own unique characteristics and applications, and understanding these differences is crucial for their effective use.

Next, we will delve into the applications of Theta Functions. We will explore how Theta Functions are used in the study of elliptic curves, which are curves defined by the equation $y^2 = x^3 + ax + b$. Elliptic curves have been studied extensively in number theory, and Theta Functions play a crucial role in their study. We will also explore how Theta Functions are used in the study of modular forms, which are mathematical functions that are defined on the upper half-plane and have certain transformation properties.

Finally, we will explore some of the more advanced applications of Theta Functions, including their use in statistical mechanics and cryptography. In statistical mechanics, Theta Functions are used to describe the distribution of particles in a system, while in cryptography, they are used in the construction of certain types of cryptographic keys.

Throughout the chapter, we will use the powerful mathematical language of complex numbers and functions, and will make extensive use of the powerful tools of complex analysis. By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of Theta Functions and their applications, and will be equipped with the knowledge to explore these fascinating topics further.




#### 7.2b Properties and Theorems

In this section, we will explore some key properties and theorems related to crossing numbers. These properties and theorems will provide a deeper understanding of the concepts introduced in the previous section.

#### Properties of Crossing Numbers

1. **Monotonicity:** The crossing number of a set of points and combinatorial curves is monotone increasing in the number of points. This means that adding more points to the set can only increase the crossing number.

2. **Subset Property:** If a set of points and combinatorial curves has a crossing number of $k$, then any subset of this set has a crossing number at most $k$.

3. **Symmetry:** The crossing number of a set of points and combinatorial curves is symmetric. This means that if the roles of points and curves are reversed, the crossing number remains the same.

#### Theorems Related to Crossing Numbers

1. **Pach-Sharir Theorem:** The Pach-Sharir Theorem provides an upper bound on the crossing number of a set of points and combinatorial curves. This theorem is a fundamental result in the study of crossing numbers and is used to prove many other theorems.

2. **Khinchine-Tammes Theorem:** The Khinchine-Tammes Theorem provides a lower bound on the crossing number of a set of points and combinatorial curves. This theorem is used in conjunction with the Pach-Sharir Theorem to determine the exact value of the crossing number in certain cases.

3. **Erdős-Szekeres Theorem:** The Erdős-Szekeres Theorem provides a lower bound on the crossing number of a set of points and combinatorial curves. This theorem is used in conjunction with the Pach-Sharir Theorem to determine the exact value of the crossing number in certain cases.

#### Applications of Crossing Numbers

Crossing numbers have a wide range of applications in various areas of mathematics. They are used in the design and analysis of algorithms, in the study of geometric combinatorics, and in many other areas. It provides a powerful tool for understanding and controlling the complexity of geometric problems. Its applications are vast and varied, making it an essential topic for anyone studying geometric combinatorics.

## Chapter 8: Theta Functions

### Introduction

In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of Theta Functions, a fundamental concept in the field of geometric combinatorics. Theta functions, named after the Greek letter 'θ', are mathematical functions that have been studied extensively in number theory, statistical mechanics, and combinatorics. They are particularly important in the study of lattices and quadratic forms, which are central to many areas of mathematics.

Theta functions are defined as the quotient of two theta series, which are series expansions of the theta function. The theta series is a power series that is defined in terms of a quadratic form and a lattice. The lattice, in this context, is a discrete set of points in a multi-dimensional space that are evenly spaced and arranged in a regular pattern.

The study of theta functions is not just a theoretical exercise. They have practical applications in various fields. For instance, in number theory, theta functions are used to study the distribution of primes and the properties of quadratic forms. In statistical mechanics, they are used to study the behavior of many-body systems. In combinatorics, they are used to study the properties of lattices and the incidence of points and lines in geometric configurations.

In this chapter, we will explore the properties of theta functions, their applications, and their role in geometric combinatorics. We will also discuss the theta series and its relationship with the theta function. By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of theta functions and their importance in the study of geometric combinatorics.

### Subsection: 7.3a Technique Overview

The Embedding Technique is a powerful tool in the study of geometric combinatorics, particularly in the context of the Pach-Sharir Theorem on Incidences of Points and Combinatorial Curves. This technique allows us to embed a given set of points and curves into a higher-dimensional space, where the incidence problem becomes simpler to analyze.

#### The Embedding Technique

The Embedding Technique is a method of transforming a set of points and curves in a plane into a set of points and curves in a higher-dimensional space. This transformation is achieved by mapping each point and curve in the plane to a point and curve in the higher-dimensional space. The key to this technique is the choice of the mapping function, which determines the properties of the embedded set.

The Embedding Technique is particularly useful in the context of the Pach-Sharir Theorem. By embedding the set of points and curves into a higher-dimensional space, we can reduce the incidence problem to a simpler form, which can then be analyzed using the tools of geometric combinatorics.

#### The Pach-Sharir Theorem

The Pach-Sharir Theorem is a fundamental result in the field of geometric combinatorics. It provides a bound on the number of incidences between a set of points and a set of combinatorial curves. The theorem is particularly useful in the study of geometric structures, such as arrangements of lines and circles.

The Embedding Technique plays a crucial role in the proof of the Pach-Sharir Theorem. By embedding the set of points and curves into a higher-dimensional space, we can reduce the incidence problem to a simpler form, which can then be analyzed using the tools of geometric combinatorics.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the Embedding Technique and its applications in the study of geometric combinatorics. We will also explore the Pach-Sharir Theorem in more detail, and discuss its implications for the study of geometric structures.
